Eschatological Beliefs in the Conclusion
Sanusi lists eschatological beliefs and notes their detailed exposition is found elsewhere.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key eschatological beliefs that Sanusi enumerates in his conclusion, including the questioning of the grave, the Ṣirāṭ, the Mīzān, the Ḥawḍ, intercession, and the eternity of Paradise and Hell.

Core Idea
The afterlife realities are as certain as the principles of tawḥīd.

Sanusi concludes his creedal work by affirming the essential beliefs about the afterlife that every Muslim must hold. These samiyyāt are not optional details but integral to sound faith. In this passage, he lists them concisely, reminding the reader that the full details are found in the books of fiqh and hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of beliefs in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the sequence of beliefs mentioned.
2
Identify each belief mentioned
List each eschatological reality: questioning of the grave, Ṣirāṭ, Mīzān, Ḥawḍ, intercession, eternal bliss and torment.
These are all part of the samiyyāt category.
3
Note the author's comment on details
Sanusi says the detailed knowledge of these matters is found in the books of fiqh and hadith. This shows the scope of his work.
The 'Umm al-Barahin only covers essential principles.
4
Reflect on why these are in a creedal work
Ask yourself why a work on tawḥīd includes these afterlife beliefs. Consider the importance of affirming what is unseen.
These beliefs are part of the fundamental creed (aqīda).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونحوِهِ مِنْ سؤالِ القبرِ ونعيمِهِ وعذابِهِ ، والصراط . والميزان ، والحوضِ ، والشفاعة للعصاة المؤمنين في إنقاذهم مِنَ النارِ بعد نفوذ الوعيدِ في جماعة منهم إجماعاً ، وتأبيد نعيم المؤمنين وعذابِ الكافرين .
6
ومعرفة تفاصيل ما أتى بهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ مُبيَّنٌ في كتب الأئمةِ مِنَ الفقه والحديث .
عن
والقَصْدُ بهذهِ العُجالةِ إنَّما هو ذكرُ ما يَخرجُ بِهِ المكلَّفُ التقليدِ في العقائدِ ، وفَهْمُ هذهِ الجُمَلِ وافٍ بذلكَ إِنْ يسَّرَ الله سبحانَهُ أَتمَّ وفاء .
وهو جلَّ وعلا المستعانُ والمسؤولُ أنْ يخرجنا بفضلِهِ ويُخرج بنا مِنَ الظلماتِ إلى النور ، وأن يكرمنا ويكرم على أيدينا بما يُوجِبُ لنا ولأحبَّتِنا مِنَ التنعم في أعلى الفردوس بشريف معرفتِهِ ولذيذ رؤيتِهِ أعظم سرورٍ ، وصلَّى الله على سيّدِنا ومولانا محمد عدد ما ذكرَهُ الذاكرونَ ، وغفل عن ذكره الغافلونَ ، ورضي الله تعالى عن أصحاب رسولِ اللهِ صلى الله عليهِ وسلَّمَ أجمعين ، والحمد لله رب العالمين .
And similar to it from the questioning of the grave, its bliss and its torment, and the Ṣirāṭ (path), and the Mīzān (scale), and the Ḥawḍ (basin), and the intercession for sinful believers to save them from the Fire after the threat has taken effect upon a group of them by consensus, and the eternal bliss of believers and the eternal torment of disbelievers. And knowledge of the details of what he (peace be upon him) brought is explained in the books of the imams from fiqh and hadith. And the purpose of this brief work is only to mention that by which the legally responsible person emerges from taqlīd in beliefs, and understanding these sentences suffices for that, if Allah the Glorious grants complete fulfillment. And He, Exalted and High, is the One we seek help from and the One we ask to bring us out by His grace and bring others out through us from darkness to light, and to honor us and honor through us what necessitates for us and our loved ones the enjoyment in the highest Paradise through the noble knowledge of Him and the delightful vision of Him — the greatest joy. And may Allah bless our Master and Patron Muhammad as many times as those who remember remember Him, and those who are heedless are heedless of His remembrance. And may Allah be pleased with all the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the book. It first completes the enumeration of eschatological beliefs (samiyyāt) that the mukallaf must hold: the questioning of the grave, its bliss and torment; the Ṣirāṭ (bridge over Hell); the Mīzān (weighing of deeds); the Ḥawḍ (basin of the Prophet); the intercession (shafā‘a) that will save sinful believers from the Fire, taking effect after some have already suffered punishment — this by consensus; and the eternal nature of both Paradise and Hell. It then notes that detailed knowledge of everything the Prophet brought is found in the books of fiqh and hadith. The author states that the purpose of this brief work ('ujāla) is only to present the essential beliefs that free the mukallaf from blind imitation (taqlīd) in matters of creed, and understanding these concise statements (jumal) suffices for that end. Finally, the passage closes with a prayer asking Allah to bring the author and his readers from darkness to light, to grant them and their loved ones the highest Paradise, the knowledge and vision of Allah as the greatest joy, and invokes blessings on the Prophet and his Companions.

Key Terms
سؤال القبر
الصراط
الميزان
الحوض
الشفاعة
تأبيد نعيم المؤمنين
تأبيد عذاب الكافرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think these eschatological beliefs are optional or less important than tawḥīd.
Correction: Sanusi includes them in a creedal work, showing they are essential to sound faith. They are part of the samiyyāt which must be affirmed.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned in the passage as one of the eschatological beliefs?
A) The questioning of the grave
B) The Ṣirāṭ
C) The Mīzān
D) The recording of deeds (Kātibān)

Key Takeaway
The eschatological beliefs are crucial and their detailed exposition is found in the larger works of fiqh and hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage affirms the reality of the grave questioning. Is your life today a sincere preparation for that moment? What will you change starting now?
?
Intercession is for 'sinful believers after the threat has taken effect on a group of them.' Does this lead you to take your sins lightly, or does it drive you to repentance before you are among those who require such intercession?
?
The lesson notes that the details of these beliefs are explained in fiqh and hadith books. Will you commit to studying one specific detail from those sources this week to strengthen your certainty? If not, what is stopping you?

Purpose and Closing Prayer
Sanusi states the book's purpose and offers a closing supplication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the stated purpose of Sanusi's brief work and the content of his closing supplication for guidance and divine vision.

Core Idea
The book's sole aim is to free the believer from blind imitation in creed.

After affirming the core beliefs, Sanusi now clarifies the precise objective of his 'Umm al-Barahin: to provide the minimal creedal knowledge that lifts the believer from taqlid (blind imitation) to intellectual conviction. He then concludes with a beautiful prayer asking for illumination and the highest vision of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on purpose and prayer
Read the Arabic and translation of the final part. Notice how the purpose statement and supplication are connected.
The purpose is practical: to exit taqlid in beliefs.
2
Analyze the purpose statement
Sanusi says the purpose is to mention what frees the mukallaf from taqlid in beliefs. Understanding these sentences suffices.
This shows the book is concise and sufficient.
3
Examine the closing supplication
Identify what Sanusi asks for: to be brought from darkness to light, honor, the highest Paradise, knowledge and vision of Allah.
The vision of Allah (ru'ya) is the greatest joy.
4
Reflect on the final blessings
The prayer ends with blessings on the Prophet and Companions, emphasizing gratitude and connection to the early community.
This is a standard closing in classical texts.

Source Text

And may Allah be pleased with all the Companions of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds.

What is the stated purpose of this brief work (al-'ujāla)?
A) To enumerate all details of fiqh and hadith
B) To mention what frees the mukallaf from taqlid in beliefs
C) To prove the existence of God
D) To refute heretical sects

Key Takeaway
The aim of 'Umm al-Barahin is to provide the minimal creedal knowledge for sound belief, and the author's prayer reflects the ultimate goal: knowledge and vision of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you still blindly following the beliefs you inherited from your family without personal conviction? What is one specific step you can take today to verify your creed using evidence from revelation and reason?
?
If the greatest joy is the vision of Allah, why do you spend more time pursuing worldly pleasures than seeking that vision? What will you change in your daily routine to prioritize knowledge of Allah?
?
Sanusi prays that Allah brings others out of darkness through him. Whom are you actively helping to come out of ignorance about Islam? Name one person you will speak to about faith this week and what you will say.

The Doubter's State in the Grave
Describes the fate of one who dies in doubt without seeking knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual danger of neglecting to resolve doubts and how the grave exposes a person's true inner state.

Core Idea
Doubt left untreated leads to ruin in the grave.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin describes the grave's exposure of one's actual knowledge and faith. It warns that a person who experiences doubts but fails to seek knowledge dies in a state of uncertainty, and the grave forces them to speak the truth of their heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the narrative flow from life to grave come through.
Notice the repetition of 'لا أدري' (I do not know) in life and in the grave.
2
Identify the two outcomes
Contrast the 'arif (knower) who speaks the truth with the doubter who can only say 'I do not know'.
The grave reveals what the person truly has.
3
Reflect on the cause
The doubter's state is due to his inaction during life: he was struck by doubt but did not investigate or treat the sickness of his heart.
The phrase 'ولا يبحث عليه' means he did not seek to resolve it.
4
Internalize the warning
The passage ends with regret when it is too late and perdition. Ask yourself: what does this teach about the urgency of seeking knowledge?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المضيق حينَ لا فكر ، فيُشكِّكُهُ في دينِهِ ، فيموتُ على شكّهِ والعياذُ بالله تعالى . وإذا كانَ في القبرِ خُتِمَ على الأفواه ، ونطق بما عندَهُ ؛ فإِنْ كانَ عارفاً نطق بالحقِّ ، وإنْ كانَ شاكاً غير عالم قال : لا أدري ، وكذا كانَ في حياتِهِ يقولُ بقلبِهِ : لا أدري ، وكانَ يطرقه الشكُ أحياناً ولا يبحث عليه ، ولا يداوي سَقام سريرته ، فإذا مات لحقه الندم حينَ لا ينفعه ، واعتذرَ إلى مَنْ لا يسمعُهُ ، وهلك والعياذُ باللهِ مِنْ سخط اللهِ تعالى
The narrowness when there is no thought, so it makes him doubt his religion, and he dies in his doubt — and we seek refuge with Allah Almighty. And when he is in the grave, mouths are sealed, and he speaks according to what he has. If he was a knower, he speaks the truth; and if he was a doubter not knowing, he says: 'I do not know.' And likewise in his life he used to say in his heart: 'I do not know,' and doubt used to strike him sometimes and he did not investigate it, nor did he treat the sickness of his innermost self. Then when he dies, regret befalls him when it does not benefit him, and he apologizes to one who does not hear him, and he perishes — and we seek refuge with Allah from the wrath of Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage describes the spiritual danger of neglecting to seek knowledge when doubt arises. The author explains that a person who experiences doubt but does not engage in reflection (fikr) dies in a state of doubt. In the grave, the person's true condition is exposed: if they were a true knower (arif), they speak the truth; if they were a doubter (shakk), they can only say 'I do not know,' reflecting their inner state during life. The passage warns that such a person neglected to treat the sickness of their heart, and after death, regret is futile.

Key Terms
شك
فكر
قبر
عارف
لا أدري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that simply saying 'I believe' verbally is enough for salvation, even if doubts remain in the heart.
Correction: This passage shows that the grave reveals the true state of the heart: the doubter can only say 'I do not know', reflecting his inner uncertainty. Genuine faith requires resolving doubts through knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the person who was a doubter say in the grave according to this passage?
A) 'I believe.'
B) 'I do not know.'
C) 'I repent.'
D) 'I was mistaken.'

Key Takeaway
Neglecting to seek knowledge when doubt arises leads to an exposed state of ruin in the grave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When a doubt about your faith creeps into your heart, do you rush to investigate it, or do you let it sit and fester? What will you do differently tomorrow?
?
You say 'I don't know' in your heart about certain matters. Can you honestly claim before Allah that you have sought knowledge to remove that 'I don't know'? If not, what is stopping you?
?
Imagine your tongue silenced in the grave and your soul forced to speak what you truly hold. Would your innermost speech be 'I know my Lord' or 'I don't know'? How will you change the answer before it is too late?

Parable of the Good and Evil Word
Interprets the parable of the good and evil tree in Surah Ibrahim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quranic parable of the good and evil tree illustrates the nature of faith and disbelief, and the centrality of the heart's conviction.

Core Idea
Faith has firm roots in the heart; disbelief is rootless.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary unpacks the profound Quranic metaphor of the good and evil tree. By interpreting the 'good word' as the testification of faith (shahada), the author shows that true faith is not merely verbal but deeply anchored in the heart. Let's read the source directly.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses and their interpretation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author quotes the verses from Surah Ibrahim and then immediately gives the interpretation by 'the people of truth'.
This is a classic tafsir approach: verse then commentary.
2
Identify the two words and their characteristics
The passage contrasts a 'good word' (kalima tayyiba) and an 'evil word' (kalima khabitha). Note the attributes: the good word has a firm root in the heart and is heard on the tongue, while the evil word has no stability in the heart.
The heart is the key battleground.
3
Reflect on the relationship between heart, tongue, and deeds
The commentary states that deeds ascend and conditions are rectified through the good word. This shows that inner conviction (heart) must precede outer action (tongue and deeds). Ask yourself: why does the author emphasize the heart?
This connects to the broader theme of faith being both inward and outward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ضربَ الله العظيم لذلكَ مثلاً فقالَ : ضَرَبَ اللهُ مَثَلًا كَلِمَةً طَيِّبَةً كَشَجَرَةِ طَيِّبَةٍ أَصْلُهَا ثَابِتُ وَفَرْعُهَا فِي السَّمَاء ) [إبراهيم : ٢٤] قال أهلُ الحقِّ : الكلمة الطيبةُ : لا إله إلا الله ، محمدٌ رسولُ اللهِ أصلُها ثابت في القلب ، وهي مسموعة في اللسان ، وهي التي تصعد بسببها الأعمال ، وتصلح بها الأحوال وقال تعالى : ﴿ وَمَثَلُ كَلِمَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ كَشَجَرَةٍ خَبِيثَةٍ أُجْتُنَّتْ مِن فَوْقِ الْأَرْضِ مَا لَهَا مِن قَرَارِ) [إبراهيم : (٢٦) ، وهي كلمة الكفر ، لا ثبوت لها في القلب
And Allah the Almighty made a parable for that, saying: 'Allah has set forth a parable: a good word like a good tree, its root firm and its branch in the sky' [Ibrahim 14:24]. The people of truth said: The good word is 'there is no god but Allah, Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah'; its root is firm in the heart, and it is heard on the tongue, and it is that by which deeds ascend and conditions are rectified. And the Most High said: 'And the parable of an evil word is like an evil tree uprooted from above the earth, having no stability' [Ibrahim 14:26], and it is the word of disbelief; it has no firmness in the heart.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage interprets the Quranic parable of the good tree and the evil tree. The 'good word' (kalima tayyiba) is identified by the people of truth (ahl al-haqq) as the shahada: 'La ilaha illa Allah, Muhammadun Rasul Allah.' Its root is firmly established in the heart, and it is uttered by the tongue. Through this word, good deeds ascend and one's spiritual state is corrected. The 'evil word' (kalima khabitha) is the word of disbelief (kufr), which has no firmness in the heart, just as an evil tree is uprooted from the earth. This emphasizes that true faith is based on inner conviction and knowledge, while disbelief lacks a solid foundation.

Key Terms
كلمة طيبة
كلمة خبيثة
لا إله إلا الله
شجرة
قلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the 'good word' is only a verbal utterance without inner conviction.
Correction: The passage stresses that the root of the good word is 'firm in the heart', indicating that inner faith is essential and prior to outward declaration.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'good word' (al-kalima al-tayyiba)?
A) Any dhikr of Allah.
B) 'La ilaha illa Allah, Muhammadun Rasul Allah.'
C) The Quran.
D) The testimony of faith without conditions.

Key Takeaway
True faith (iman) must be firmly rooted in the heart; without inner conviction, words and deeds lack foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you feel its roots gripping your heart, or is it just a phrase on your tongue? What would need to change for your faith to feel as solid as a tree with deep roots?
?
If your iman were a tree, where would you find the strongest roots—and where are the shallow ones that might be uprooted by a trial? Be honest: what area of your life reveals the weakest anchor?
?
Your deeds ascend only because of the good word rooted in your heart. If your deeds feel light or empty, could it be that the root is weak? What will you do today to sink your faith deeper into your heart, not just your speech?

The Analogy of the King: Proximity and Knowledge
Sanusi expounds an analogy of a king to illustrate the Companions' knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses an analogy of a king to demonstrate the unique knowledge of the Companions due to their proximity to the Prophet.

Core Idea
Proximity to the Prophet gave the Companions unparalleled knowledge of Allah, like a king's intimate.

Sanusi begins by presenting a rational analogy that appeals to common experience. He compares the Companions' relationship to the Prophet to that of people who sit with someone close to a king. Through this analogy, he establishes a foundational principle: closeness to the one who knows the king best yields profound knowledge. This lesson unpacks that analogy and its application to the Sahaba.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the analogy carefully. Notice how the author sets up the scenario of a king and his intimate.
The passage starts with an ellipsis indicating previous context; focus on the given analogy.
2
Identify the logical steps
Break down the analogy: (a) there is a king, (b) a person close to the king, (c) others sit with that person. Why do they become knowledgeable about the king? The text says they become like observers because of the strength of knowledge from the intimate.
The key phrase is 'في حكم المشاهدين للملك'.
3
Apply to the Companions
See how Sanusi applies this: The Prophet is the closest to Allah, and the Companions sat with him. Therefore, they attain powerful knowledge of Allah.
Note the phrase 'فافهم بهذا المثالِ قوة معرفة الصحابة'.
4
Reflect on the power of the analogy
Consider why this analogy is effective in conveying the Companions' status. It uses a familiar social hierarchy to explain spiritual proximity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الناس إليه . . لكانَ أصحابُ ذلك الإنسان المجالسونَ لهُ أعرف بالمَلِكِ مِنْ غيرهم وإن لم يكونوا شاهدوا المَلِكَ ، وأنهم يصيرون في المعرفة به لقربهم مِنَ الأقرب إليه ومجالستهم له في حكم المشاهدين للمَلِكِ ؛ لقوَّة معرفتهم به .
فافهم بهذا المثالِ قوَّة معرفة الصحابة رضى الله تعالى عنهم لمولاهم جلَّ وعزَّ ، الذي أهَلَهم لمجالسة أكرم الخلْقِ عليه وأقربهم لديهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، بل أثنى عليهم مولانا جل وعزَّ في القرآنِ ثناءً عظيماً يُؤذِنُ بعلو مكانتهم عندَهُ ، وعظيم قربهم منه ، وأثنى عليهم نبينا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ وعَظَّمهم ؛ فقال تعالى مُحَمَّدٌ رَسُولُ اللهِ وَالَّذِينَ مَعَهُ . . . ﴾ [الفتح : ٢٩] الآية .
وقالَ : وَالسَّبِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ . . . [التوبة : ١٠٠] الآية . وقالَ : لَقَدْ رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ إِذْ يُبَايِعُونَكَ تَحْتَ الشَّجَرَةِ . . . [الفتح : : ١٨] الآية .
وقالَ : رِجَالٌ صَدَقُوا مَا عَهَدُوا اللَّهَ عَلَيْهِ ...) [الأحزاب : ٢٣) الآية . وقال صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : ( أَقْتَدُوا بِاللَّذَيْنِ مِنْ بَعْدِي : أَبِي بَكْرٍ « وَعُمَرَ (١) .
وقالَ : ( أَصْحَابِي كَالنُّجُوم ، بِأَيْهِمُ افْتَدَيْتُمُ اهْتَدَيْتُمْ (۲) «
وقالَ : « مَثَلُ أَصْحَابِي كَمَثَلِ الْمِلْحِ فِي الطَّعَامِ ، لَا يَصْلُحُ الطَّعَامُ إلا به (( ۱)
.
وقالَ : « اللَّهَ اللَّهَ فِي أَصْحَابِي ، لا تَتَّخِذُوهُمْ غَرَضاً مِنْ بَعْدِي ؛ فَمَنْ أَحَبَّهُمْ فَبِحُبِّي أَحَبَّهُمْ ، وَمَنْ أَبْغَضَهُمْ فَبِبُغْضِي أَبْغَضَهُمْ ، وَمَنْ آذَاهُمْ فَقَدْ آذَانِي ، وَمَنْ آذَانِي فَقَدْ أَذَى اللَّهَ ، وَمَنْ أَذَى اللَّهَ سُبْحَانَهُ يُوشِكُ أَنْ يَأْخُذَهُ » (۲)
وقال : ( لا تَسُبُّوا أَصْحَابِي ، فَلَوْ أَنْفَقَ أَحَدُكُمْ مِثْلَ أُحُدٍ ذَهَباً مَا بَلَغَ مُدَّ أَحَدِهِمْ وَلَا نَصِيفَهُ (۳) .
وقالَ : ( مَنْ سَبَّ أَصْحَابِي فَعَلَيْهِ لَعْنَةُ اللَّهِ وَالْمَلَائِكَةِ وَالنَّاسِ أَجْمَعِينَ ، وَلا يَقْبَلُ اللهُ مِنْهُ صَرْفاً وَلا عَدْلاً (٤)
People to him... so that the companions of that person who sit with him become more knowledgeable about the king than others, even if they have not seen the king, and they become in knowledge of him due to their closeness to the one closest to him and their sitting with him as if they are among the observers of the king because of the strength of their knowledge of him. So understand by this example the strength of the knowledge of the companions (radiya Allahu ta'ala anhum) of their Lord (jalla wa 'azza), who qualified them to sit with the most honored of creation with Him and the closest to Him (salla Allahu 'alayhi wa sallam). Rather, our Lord (jalla wa 'azza) praised them in the Qur'an with great praise indicating the loftiness of their station with Him and the greatness of their closeness to Him, and our Prophet and Master Muhammad (salla Allahu 'alayhi wa sallam) praised them and magnified them. So He said, exalted: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah, and those who are with him...' [al-Fath:29] the verse. And He said: 'And the foremost, the first...' [at-Tawba:100] the verse. And He said: 'Certainly Allah was pleased with the believers when they pledged allegiance to you under the tree...' [al-Fath:18] the verse. And He said: 'Men who fulfilled their covenant with Allah...' [al-Ahzab:23] the verse. And the Prophet (salla Allahu 'alayhi wa sallam) said: 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar.' (1) And he said: 'My companions are like stars; by whichever of them you follow, you will be guided.' (2) And he said: 'The example of my companions is like that of salt in food; food is only good with it.' (1) And he said: 'Fear Allah regarding my companions, do not make them targets after me; whoever loves them loves me, and whoever hates them hates me, and whoever harms them harms me, and whoever harms me harms Allah, and whoever harms Allah, He will soon seize him.' (2) And he said: 'Do not insult my companions, for if one of you were to spend the equivalent of Uhud in gold, he would not reach the measure of one of them nor half of it.' (3) And he said: 'Whoever insults my companions, upon him is the curse of Allah, the angels, and all people, and Allah will not accept from him any exchange or justice.' (4)
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part proof of the exalted status of the Prophet's companions (Sahaba). The author first presents an intuitive analogy: if someone is close to a king, even a person who has never seen the king but sits with that king's intimate becomes more knowledgeable about the king than others, because of the intimate's proximity and knowledge. The author then applies this to the Sahaba: their proximity to the Prophet (the most honored of creation and closest to Allah) gives them superior knowledge of Allah. This is not just a claim; the author immediately supports it with divine and prophetic testimony. He quotes four Qur'anic verses: the first from Surat al-Fath (48:29) praising the general body of the companions, the second from Surat at-Tawba (9:100) praising the early emigrants and helpers, the third from Surat al-Fath (48:18) mentioning Allah's pleasure with the believers under the tree, and the fourth from Surat al-Ahzab (33:23) commending those who fulfilled their covenant. Then he cites a series of hadith: the instruction to follow Abu Bakr and 'Umar, the statement that the companions are like stars guiding people, the analogy of salt for the indispensability of the companions, a stern warning against harming them, the prohibition of insulting them with the illustration that no amount of gold can match their merit, and a severe curse upon those who revile them. The overall argument moves from a rational analogy to scriptural confirmation, establishing that the Sahaba's virtue is both rationally plausible and divinely attested.

Key Terms
الصحابة
المثال
المعرفة
الملك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the analogy, why do those who sit with the king's intimate become more knowledgeable about the king?
A) They have seen the king directly.
B) The intimate gives them detailed descriptions.
C) Their closeness to the intimate gives them certainty like direct observation.
D) They have magical insight.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' proximity to the Prophet gave them a unique and powerful knowledge of Allah, as illustrated by the king analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions gained unmatched knowledge of Allah simply by sitting with the Prophet. Who are the people in your life that bring you closer to Allah? Are you intentionally seeking their company, or are you content with distance? What one step will you take this week to sit with someone who reminds you of Allah?
?
If you truly believed that sitting with a person could give you knowledge of Allah as if you had seen Him, would you prioritize that over other activities? The Companions did. What is holding you back from seeking out such company?
?
The passage says the Companions' knowledge came through the Prophet's presence. Today, we have the Quran and Sunnah – they are the Prophet's legacy. When you read them, do you feel the same urgency and closeness as if the Prophet were speaking directly to you? Or do you read them as distant texts? What would change in your study if you approached every verse as a personal address from your Lord?

Scriptural Proofs: Qur'an and Hadith on the Companions' Rank
Sanusi cites Qur'anic verses and hadiths to confirm the Companions' exalted rank.

Learning Objective
This lesson establishes the divine and prophetic testimony to the exalted status of the Companions, as cited by Sanusi.

Core Idea
Both the Qur'an and the Prophet himself affirm the lofty rank of the Companions.

After presenting the rational analogy, Sanusi reinforces his argument with explicit texts from the Qur'an and authentic hadiths. These sources leave no doubt about the Companions' high station. In this lesson, we examine each proof text and see how they collectively confirm the analogy's conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the scriptural passages
Go through the four Qur'anic verses and multiple hadiths cited. Notice the diversity: praise, pleasure, covenant, and explicit commands.
There are verses from al-Fath, at-Tawba, and al-Ahzab.
2
Analyze the Qur'anic verses
Each verse highlights a different aspect: 'Muhammad is the Messenger... those with him', 'the foremost among the first', 'Allah pleased with the believers under the tree', 'men who fulfilled their covenant'. Reflect on the strong language (e.g., 'pleased').
Verse references: 48:29, 9:100, 48:18, 33:23.
3
Examine the hadiths
The hadiths include explicit praise, warnings against insult, and a statement about the companions being like stars. Note the severity of the curse on those who insult them.
Hadiths about following Abu Bakr and Umar, stars, salt, love, and curse.
4
Connect to the analogy
How do these proofs fulfill the promise of the analogy? The analogy suggested they have supreme knowledge; the texts show they are praised by Allah and the Prophet.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson shows that such indifference is not neutral—it borders on failing to honor those whom Allah and His Prophet praised. Ask yourself: what concrete step will you take to better love and respect the Companions starting today?
?
The Prophet linked love of the Companions to love of himself. Examine your heart: do you truly love the Companions because they are beloved to the Prophet, or do you take them for granted? What action can you take this week to demonstrate that love—perhaps by studying their virtues or defending them when their honor is attacked?
?
Imagine standing before Allah and being asked: 'Did you honor My beloved Companions as I commanded?' Based on your current attitude and actions, would your answer bring you ease or fear? What needs to change in your daily speech and thoughts to align with the weight of the hadiths in this lesson?

The True Calamity: Neglecting Tawhid for the Afterlife
Author's reflection on the true calamity of neglecting tawhid, focusing on the grave and afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why neglecting the study of tawhid is a calamity that extends beyond worldly life to the grave and afterlife.

Core Idea
The real disaster is facing the grave and afterlife without knowledge of tawhid.

This passage is Sanusi's personal reflection on the gravity of ignoring theology. He shifts the focus from worldly misfortune to the existential crisis of the grave and the afterlife. The lesson will unpack his warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's progression sink in.
2
Identify the two calamities
Notice how Sanusi contrasts worldly humiliation with the greater calamity of the grave questioning and afterlife horrors.
The key is that worldly loss is not the real issue.
3
Understand 'good knowledge' (husn al-ma'rifa)
The passage says only those blessed with good knowledge of Allah will be saved. Reflect on what 'good knowledge' entails.
This knowledge is the goal of studying tawhid.
4
Reflect on the regret of the negligent
The passage ends with the regret of those who turned away from tawhid. Consider how this motivates the study of theology.
Regret is useless in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : ويا ليتَهُ لو كانَ حدُّ المصيبة ما نالَهُ في دنياه ، كيف وسؤالُ مُنْكَرٍ ونَكِيرِ في القبرِ عن التوحيد أمامَهُ ؟! وأهوال الآخرة التي لا ينجو منها إلا مَنْ أنعمَ اللهُ عليهِ الآنَ بحُسْنِ المعرفة بالله تعالى .. هي بالمرصاد لكلِّ أحدٍ ، وعندها يمتاز الخبيثُ مِنَ الطيب ، وتنكشفُ أسرار الجهالات في أصول العقائدِ ، ويحصد المُفرِّطُ في ذلك اليوم الندامة التي زرع أسبابها في هذه الدارِ ؛ مِنْ إعراضِهِ عَنِ النظرِ في علمِ التوحيد ، واشتغاله بما لا يعنيه ، ويتقطَّعُ حِينَئِذٍ حَسَرَاتٍ ولا ينفعُهُ ذلك
I said: Would that the limit of the calamity were what afflicted him in this world; how (can that be) while the questioning of Munkar and Nakir in the grave about tawhid is before him?! And the horrors of the afterlife, from which no one escapes except one whom God has now blessed with good knowledge of God the Exalted ... are in ambush for everyone. At that time the wicked is distinguished from the good, and the secrets of ignorances concerning the principles of beliefs are exposed, and the negligent on that day reaps the regret whose causes he sowed in this abode, due to his turning away from contemplation in the science of tawhid and occupying himself with what does not concern him. Then he will be cut up with regrets, and that will not benefit him.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is the author's own reflection (qultu: 'I said') building on the preceding quote by 'Abd al-Jalil. The author says: Would that the only calamity were the worldly humiliation described earlier; but the real disaster is the questioning by the angels Munkar and Nakir in the grave about tawhid (divine unity). Moreover, the terrors of the afterlife (أهوال الآخرة) await everyone. Only those whom God has blessed with true knowledge (حسن المعرفة بالله) will be saved. In that realm, the wicked will be separated from the good, and the ignorances concerning the principles of beliefs will be exposed. The one who neglected theology will harvest regret, having sowed its causes in this world by turning away from the study of tawhid and busying himself with irrelevant matters. He will then be cut up with regrets, but that will not help him. The passage underscores the urgency of studying 'ilm al-tawhid to prepare for the grave and the afterlife.

Key Terms
سؤال منكر ونكير
التوحيد
حسن المعرفة
أهوال الآخرة
أصول العقائد
علم التوحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the only consequence of neglecting theology is worldly humiliation.
Correction: The author stresses that the real calamity is the grave questioning and afterlife horrors, which only knowledge of tawhid can avert.

Checkpoint

A) Those who are wealthy

D) Those who are famous scholars

Key Takeaway
Neglecting the study of tawhid leads to regret in the grave and afterlife; true knowledge of Allah is the only salvation.

?
You will stand before the horrors of the afterlife, and only those with good knowledge of Allah will escape. Are you actively acquiring that knowledge, or are you hoping that general good deeds will suffice without deep understanding of who Allah is? What step will you take this week to deepen your ma'rifa?

The Metaphorical Interpretation of 'Saaq' in the Qur'an
Ibn Dahaq interprets the verse metaphorically and cites a supporting hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'anic expression 'yawma yukshafu 'an saaq' (Q. 68:42) is a metaphor for the severity of the Day of Judgment, not a literal limb of God, as explained by Ibn Dahaq and supported by a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The 'uncovering of the shin' is an idiom for extreme hardship, not a divine body part.

This lesson examines a key passage from Ibn Dahaq's commentary on the Qur'an. He addresses a verse often misunderstood by those who attribute physical limbs to God. By explaining the Arabic idiom and citing a relevant hadith, Ibn Dahaq shows that the expression refers to the awe-inspiring hardships of the Day of Resurrection. This is a classic example of how Muslim scholars interpret ambiguous Qur'anic verses in a way that upholds God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Ibn Dahaq's interpretation and the supporting hadith.
Notice how Ibn Dahaq starts with the verse and immediately clarifies its intended meaning.
2
Understand the Arabic idiom
Pay attention to the analogy with the phrase 'the war stood on its shin' – an Arabic idiom for extreme severity. Ibn Dahaq uses this to show the metaphorical nature of 'saaq'.
This idiom is key to avoiding a literal interpretation.
3
Connect the hadith to the interpretation
Reflect on how the cited hadith reinforces the metaphorical meaning by describing the events of the Day of Judgment without any reference to a physical limb.
The hadith focuses on worship of false deities and the testing of believers, aligning with the idea of severe trials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ابن دهاق في « شرح الإرشاد » لما تكلم على قوله تعالى : (يَوْمَ يُكْشَفُ عَن سَاقٍ) [القلم : 42] : المعني بالآية : الإنباء عن أحوال يوم القيامة وصعوبة أهوالها، وما يندفع إليه المجرمون من أنكالها. وإذا جد الأمر في الحرب، واستعرت الصدور بالغيظ، والتحمت المصارع... قيل: قد قامت الحرب على ساقها، ولا يتخيل حمل الساق على جارحة ذو تحصيل. وقد ورد عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حديث ظهر منه تفسير هذه الآية؛ وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إذا كان يوم القيامة نادى مناد: من كان يعبد شيئا فليتبعه؛ فمن عبد الشمس اتبعها، ومن عبد القمر اتبعه، ومن عبد الطواغيت اتبعها، وتبقى هذه الأمة وفيها منافقوها...»، وهم الذين كان الريب والشك في قلوبهم
Ibn Dahaq said in the commentary on al-Irshad, when discussing the saying of the Exalted: 'The day when the shin shall be uncovered' (Q. 68:42): The meaning of the verse is: informing about the conditions of the Day of Resurrection and the difficulty of its terrors, and what the criminals will be driven to of its fetters. When matters become serious in war, and chests blaze with rage, and battlefields become fierce... it is said: 'The war has stood on its shin (i.e., become severe),' and one who has attainment cannot imagine taking 'shin' (saaq) as a limb. And there has come from the Messenger of God (peace and blessings be upon him) a hadith by which the interpretation of this verse becomes apparent; it is his saying (peace be upon him): 'When the Day of Resurrection comes, a caller will call out: Whoever used to worship something, let him follow it. So whoever worshipped the sun follows it, whoever worshipped the moon follows it, whoever worshipped the false deities follows them, and this Ummah remains, in which are its hypocrites...' and they are those in whose hearts there is doubt and suspicion.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a tafsir (exegesis) of the Qur'anic verse 'Yawma yukshafu 'an saaq' from Surah al-Qalam. The author, Ibn Dahaq, explains that the intended meaning is not a literal physical shin but rather a metaphorical expression for the severity and hardship of the Day of Judgment. He draws an analogy with the Arabic idiom 'qamat al-harbu 'ala saaqiha' (the war stood on its shin), meaning the war has become intense. He then supports this interpretation with a prophetic hadith that describes the events of that day, where people who worshipped false objects will follow them, and the believers who remained will be tested. The hadith indicates that the 'uncovering of the shin' refers to the unveiling of the true situation, not a bodily part. The passage thus guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih) by affirming that such expressions in the Qur'an are to be understood metaphorically in a way befitting God's majesty.

Key Terms
ساق
يوم القيامة
تأويل
الأهوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'yawma yukshafu 'an saaq' is literally about God's shin being uncovered.
Correction: Muslim scholars interpret it metaphorically as the severity of the Day of Judgment, as shown by Ibn Dahaq using Arabic idiom and hadith.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Dahaq's explanation, what does the term 'saaq' in the verse 'Yawma yukshafu 'an saaq' (Q. 68:42) actually signify?
A) A bodily limb of God.
B) The severity and hardship of the Day of Judgment.
C) The leg of the believers.
D) A specific angel.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse 'Yawma yukshafu 'an saaq' is a powerful metaphor for the immense difficulty of the Day of Resurrection, not a description of a divine limb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'Yawma yukshafu 'an saaq', do you find yourself tempted to imagine a physical limb? What does that reveal about your understanding of God's transcendence? Will you commit to always seeking the metaphorical interpretation when encountering such verses?
?
The hadith describes people following their false gods on that day. Are there any attachments in your life that you are following besides God? How can you ensure that on that day you are among those who remain with the Prophet?
?
The verse speaks of the severity of the Day. How does the reality of that day influence your daily choices? Are you living as if that day is certain? What one change will you make today to prepare?

Contemplating God's Creation of Pains and the Horrors of Hell
Urges reflection on pains and describes hell's horrors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that reflecting on God's creation of pain and the horrors of hell is as important as reflecting on His blessings, and will grasp the rhetorical purpose of these vivid descriptions.

Core Idea
Reflecting on hell's horrors is as essential as reflecting on paradise's delights.

The author draws a parallel between contemplating God's gifts and His punishments. Just as believers marvel at the wonders of creation and paradise, they must also consider the terrifying realities God has prepared for the disobedient. This passage challenges us to let both hope and fear shape our faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythmic and emotional style come through.
Notice the long sentences building intensity.
2
Identify the parallel structure
Find where the author compares reflection on pleasures and pains. Mark the transition phrase 'likewise also' (كذلك أيضاً).
This establishes the principle of balanced reflection.
3
List the horrors of hell
Scan the second part and enumerate the specific terrors: levels, zabaniyya, animals, darkness, ghislin, hameem, snakes, scorpions, chains, etc.
The author uses a cumulative list to overwhelm the reader.
4
Reflect on the rhetorical questions
Note the two questions: 'So how about looking into it?!' and 'So how about dwelling in it?!' Understand their escalating force.
The questions move from observation to direct experience.
5
Consider the purpose
Ask: Why include such graphic imagery? The author aims to instill awe and fear of Allah's power, motivating obedience.
This is not literal cartography but a rhetorical call to faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما يعتبرُ المعتبرونَ بالنظر فيما خلق مولانا جل وعزَّ مِنْ أنواع اللذَّاتِ والسرور ، وعجائب السماوات والأرضين ، وما أودع في الجِنانِ مِنْ دقائقِ عجائب مُلْكِهِ ممَّا لا يُحاط بهِ ، ولا لمحته قطُّ عين ، ولا سمعَتْ بهِ قطُّ أُذُنٌ ، ولا خطر قط على قلب بشر .. كذلك أيضاً يعتبرون فيما خلق الله تعالى مِنَ الآلام ، واختلاف أنواعها ، ودقائقِ أحوالها ، وما أودع سبحانهُ في دَرَكاتِ النارِ ، نعوذُ بوجهه الكريمِ مِنْ جميعها ؛ مِنْ عجائب الأهوالِ وأجناس العذاب الخارجة عن الحصرِ ، وعظيم ذواتِ زبانيتها وحيواناتها المعدَّةِ لتعذيب أهلها ، إلى ذلك ممَّا لا يخطرُ تحتَ بال غير حد ثم انخراقِ العادةِ في إمساكِ الحياة لأهلها مع ما لا يُطاقُ سماعُهُ مِنْ أهوالها ، فكيف بالنظر فيها ؟! فكيف ـ والعياذ بالله تعالى ـ بالحلولِ فيها ؟! وإسلام الشخص الضعيف إلى ما يتلاطم ويركب بعضُهُ بعضاً ، ويضرب وجه منه في وجهِ مِنْ عظيم ظلماتها وأعناقها وألسنة لهبها ، والأمواج المتلاطمة المتطايرةِ مِنْ شدَّةِ الغليانِ مِنْ بحارِ غسلينها وحميمها ، وأطوادِ حَيَّاتِها ، وعقاربها ، وضيق آبارها وسجونها وتُخُومِها ، وثِقَل قيودها وأنكالها ومقامعها ، وصواعق أصواتها وأصوات زبانيتها التي تخلعُ القلوبَ عن أبدانِها ، إلى غير ذلكَ ممَّا لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى
And just as those who reflect consider by looking at what our Master, Glorified and Exalted, has created of the types of pleasures and delights, and the wonders of the heavens and the earths, and what He has deposited in the Gardens of the subtleties of the wonders of His kingdom—things that cannot be encompassed, no eye has ever seen, no ear has ever heard, nor has it ever occurred to a human heart—likewise also they consider what Allah Almighty has created of pains, their different types, the subtleties of their states, and what He, Exalted, has deposited in the levels of the Fire—we seek refuge in His noble Face from all of it—including the wonders of terrors, the kinds of torture beyond enumeration, the mighty beings of its keepers (zabaniyya) and its animals prepared for tormenting its inhabitants, to that which does not occur to anyone's mind at all, then the breaking of custom in preserving life for its inhabitants with what is unbearable to hear of its terrors. So how about looking into it?! So how—and may Allah protect us—about dwelling in it?! And the surrendering of a weak person to what surges and rides one over another, and strikes face to face from the vastness of its darknesses, its necks, and the tongues of its flame, and the surging waves flying from the intensity of boiling from the seas of its ghislin (foul discharge) and its hameem (scalding water), and the mountains of its snakes, its scorpions, the narrowness of its wells, prisons, and depths, the weight of its fetters, chains, and maces, the thunderous sounds and the voices of its keepers that tear hearts from bodies, and other things that no one knows except Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is an exhortatory reflection on God's creation of pains, paralleling the earlier reflection on pleasures. The author begins by noting that contemplating pleasures—the types of delights, wonders of creation, and the unseen beauties of paradise—is a common practice for those who reflect. Similarly, one should reflect on the pains God has created: their types, subtleties, and especially the torments of hell. The author then describes the horrors of hell in vivid, almost cataclysmic terms: terrors beyond count, the zabaniyya (guardians), animals for torture, the miraculous preservation of life amidst unbearable suffering, the surging darkness, boiling seas of ghislin and hameem, snakes, scorpions, narrow prisons, heavy chains, and the thunderous voices of the keepers that rip hearts from bodies. The passage builds to a climax with rhetorical questions: how can one even look upon such things, let alone dwell in them? It concludes by noting that the full extent of hell's horrors is known only to Allah. The purpose is to instill awe, fear, and a sense of urgency in the reader to reflect on the reality of divine punishment.

الآلام
اللذات
دركات النار
زبانيتها
غسلين
حميم
أطواد الحياة
مقامع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The vivid descriptions are a literal scientific map of hell's geography.
Correction: The passage uses rhetorical hyperbole to instill awe and fear, not to provide a factual layout. The exact details are in Allah's knowledge alone.

Checkpoint

A) To frighten people unnecessarily.
B) To demonstrate God's power and encourage reflection and fear of His punishment.
C) To provide a literal map of hell.
D) To argue that punishment is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Reflecting on hell's horrors is a vital act of faith, driving believers to seek Allah's mercy and avoid His displeasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says these things 'do not occur to anyone's mind.' Given that you now have some awareness, what is your responsibility? How will you ensure that this knowledge leads to action rather than just an emotional reaction?

Balanced Reflection: Contemplating Mercy and Wrath
Devotional reflection on God's creation of delights and punishments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of balanced reflection on God's creation of both pleasures and pains, and the reality of Hell's torments as a basis for seeking God's mercy.

Core Idea
Reflecting on both mercy and wrath deepens faith and drives supplication.

This passage from Sanusi's 'Aqidah al-Sughra is a powerful devotional exhortation that calls the believer to contemplate both the delights of Paradise and the terrors of Hell. It grounds abstract theological beliefs in vivid imagery, leading to a heartfelt prayer. As you read, notice how the author moves from general principles to specific descriptions, then to personal supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage as a whole
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Notice the shift from description to prayer.
2
Identify the two poles of reflection
The author contrasts the reflection on pleasures (Paradise) with reflection on pains (Hell). Mark where this shift occurs.
3
Analyze the description of Hell
Focus on the phrase 'انخراق العادة' and why the author emphasizes the preservation of life. Then, observe how the description leads to the supplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عِبْرُ لمَنْ تأَمَّلَ فِي خَلْقِ اللهِ تعالى للآلام وكما يعتبرُ المعتبرونَ بالنظر فيما خلق مولانا جل وعزَّ مِنْ أنواع اللذَّاتِ والسرور ، وعجائب السماوات والأرضين ، وما أودع في الجِنانِ مِنْ دقائقِ عجائب مُلْكِهِ ممَّا لا يُحاط بهِ ، ولا لمحته قطُّ عين ، ولا سمعَتْ بهِ قطُّ أُذُنٌ ، ولا خطر قط على قلب بشر .. كذلك أيضاً يعتبرون فيما خلق الله تعالى مِنَ الآلام ، واختلاف أنواعها ، ودقائقِ أحوالها ، وما أودع سبحانهُ في دَرَكاتِ النارِ ، نعوذُ بوجهه الكريمِ مِنْ جميعها ؛ مِنْ عجائب الأهوالِ وأجناس العذاب الخارجة عن الحصرِ ، وعظيم ذواتِ زبانيتها وحيواناتها المعدَّةِ لتعذيب أهلها ، إلى ذلك ممَّا لا يخطرُ تحتَ بال غير حد ثم انخراقِ العادةِ في إمساكِ الحياة لأهلها مع ما لا يُطاقُ سماعُهُ مِنْ أهوالها ، فكيف بالنظر فيها ؟! فكيف ـ والعياذ بالله تعالى ـ بالحلولِ فيها ؟! وإسلام الشخص الضعيف إلى ما يتلاطم ويركب بعضُهُ بعضاً ، ويضرب وجه منه في وجهِ مِنْ عظيم ظلماتها وأعناقها وألسنة لهبها ، والأمواج المتلاطمة المتطايرةِ مِنْ شدَّةِ الغليانِ مِنْ بحارِ غسلينها وحميمها ، وأطوادِ حَيَّاتِها ، وعقاربها ، وضيق آبارها وسجونها وتُخُومِها ، وثِقَل قيودها وأنكالها ومقامعها ، وصواعق أصواتها وأصوات زبانيتها التي تخلعُ القلوبَ عن أبدانِها ، إلى غير ذلكَ ممَّا لا يعلمه إلا الله تعالى اللهمَّ فسبحانَ مَنْ أمسكَ تلك الأرواحَ لمقاساة تلكَ الشدائدِ كلَّها ، يا مَنْ إليه ملجأُ كلِّ مكروب ، وعلى سَعَةِ رحمتِهِ يُعوَّلُ في نيل كلٌّ مطلوب ؛ عاملنا يا مولانا دنيا وأخرى بمخضِ فضلك ، واكفنا يا أرحم الراحمين هم الدنيا وعذاب الآخرة ، في عافية بلا محنة ، بجميلِ مَنْكَ وعظيمٍ طَوْلِكَ ، واسمح يا ذا الجلال والإكرام بلا مؤاخذة لأبوينا وآبائهما وأمَّهاتِهما ، ولإخواننا ومَنْ نُحِبُّهُ أو يحبُّنا لوجهكَ الكريم ، واجمع شَمْلَ جميعنا يا مولانا في الفردوس الأعلى معَ خاصةِ أوليائِكَ وأهل معرفتِك ، بمخض كرمك وجودِكَ يا علي يا عظيم ، يا حليم يا عليم ؛ نتوسَّلُ إليكَ في نيل هذا المطلب الأسنى بمَنْ مَنَنْتَ علينا بالإيمانِ بهِ ؛ أفضل خلقِكَ ، الشفيع المشفّع عندَكَ ؛ سيِّدِنا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ، مَا نِيلَ بالتوسُّلِ بِهِ إلى مولانا جل وعلا كلُّ خيرٍ عميم
A lesson for those who reflect on God's creation of pains. And just as the thoughtful consider by looking at what our Master, High and Mighty, created from the types of pleasures and delights, and the wonders of the heavens and earths, and what He placed in the Gardens from the delicate wonders of His kingdom, which cannot be encompassed, which no eye has glimpsed, no ear has heard, and no human heart has conceived—so also they consider what God created from pains, their different types, the subtleties of their states, and what He, Glorified, placed in the depths of the Fire (we seek refuge in His Noble Face from all of it): from the wonders of terrors and kinds of punishment beyond count, the enormity of its keepers (Zabaniya) and the animals prepared for tormenting its inhabitants, up to that which cannot occur to a mind without limit. Then the breaking of custom in preserving life for its inhabitants with what cannot be borne to hear of its terrors—how then about looking at it? How then—and we seek refuge with God—about dwelling in it? And the surrender of a weak person to that which surges and rides one upon another, and strikes its face into the face of its great darknesses, its necks, its tongues of flame, the surging, flying waves from the intensity of boiling from the seas of its pus and boiling water, the mountains of its snakes and its scorpions, the narrowness of its pits, prisons, and depths, the weight of its fetters, chains, and iron hammers, the thunderous sounds of its voices and the voices of its keepers that tear hearts from bodies, and other things that none knows except God. O God! So glory be to the One who preserved those souls to endure all those hardships! O He to Whom is the refuge of every distressed one, and on the expanse of His mercy is relied for attaining every desired thing. Deal with us, O our Master, in this world and the next, with the unfailing generosity of Your favor, and spare us, O Most Merciful of the merciful, the worries of this world and the torment of the Hereafter, in wellbeing without trial, by Your beautiful gift and great bounty. And forgive, O Possessor of Majesty and Honor, without reproach, our parents and their fathers and mothers, and our brothers and those whom we love or who love us for Your Noble Face. And gather all of us, O our Master, in the Highest Paradise with the elect of Your saints and people of Your knowledge, by the overflow of Your generosity and bounty. O Mighty, O Great, O Forbearing, O All-Knowing; we beseech You in attaining this highest goal by the one whom You have favored us with faith in him: the best of Your creation, the intercessor whose intercession is accepted with You, our Master Muhammad, may God bless him and grant him peace. Nothing has been attained by seeking intercession through him to our Master, High and Mighty, except all abundant good.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a devotional exhortation that begins by describing the proper method of reflection for those who take heed. The author states that the thoughtful reflect on God's creation of various types of pleasures and delights, and the wonders of the heavens and earths, as well as the delicate marvels of Paradise that are beyond human perception (referencing the hadith about what no eye has seen nor ear heard). Then he says that in the same way, they reflect on the pains that God created, their different types, and what He placed in the depths of Hell—terrors, punishments beyond count, the keepers (Zabaniya) and animals prepared for torment. He then emphasizes a particular miracle: the breaking of the normal custom (kharq al-'ada) in that God preserves the life of the inhabitants of Hell despite the unbearable horrors they experience. He asks rhetorically: if just hearing these terrors is unbearable, how about seeing them, and even more, being in them? He then vividly describes the physical torments: darkness, flames, boiling liquids, snakes, scorpions, narrow pits, heavy chains, thunderous voices that tear hearts. He concludes this description by stating that only God knows the full extent. Then he turns to supplication, glorifying God for preserving souls to endure such hardships, and entreating God's mercy for this world and the Hereafter, for forgiveness, and for gathering in Paradise with the righteous, through the intercession of the Prophet Muhammad. The passage thus blends doctrinal affirmation of the physical reality of Hell with a spiritual call to fear and hope, ending with a prayer.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is just emotional rhetoric with no theological significance.
Correction: It affirms the real, physical nature of Hell and the principle of divine preservation of life as a kharq al-ada (breaking of custom), which is a significant theological concept.

Checkpoint
Why does the author mention 'انخراق العادة' (breaking of custom) in relation to Hell?
A) To argue that the torments of Hell are not real
B) To highlight that God miraculously preserves life despite unbearable suffering
C) To show that Hell is only a metaphor
D) To prove that the inhabitants will eventually die

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last sinned, did you genuinely feel the terror of the Hellfire described in this passage, or did you rationalize it away as distant or metaphorical? What will you do today to make that fear real enough to change your actions?
?
The author says the thoughtful reflect on both mercy and wrath. Which one do you naturally avoid thinking about? How will you intentionally bring both to mind starting now, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
You are about to face a trial. Will you trust that God preserves life even in the fire? How can that truth transform your patience and reliance on Him in your current struggle?

The Moon Hadith: Proving Vision Without Corporeality
Sanusi cites the moon hadith and explains the correct meaning of the simile.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the hadith of the moon serves as a proof for the vision of Allah and why the simile is limited to clarity of vision, not to physical attributes.

Core Idea
The moon hadith proves ru'yah, but the comparison is about clarity, not corporeality.

Sanusi uses the well-known hadith about seeing Allah like the full moon to defend the doctrine of ru'yah. However, to prevent anthropomorphic misinterpretations, he carefully explains that the likeness is only about the lack of jostling and harm. This passage is crucial for maintaining divine transcendence while affirming the reality of vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith in Arabic and English
Start by reading the Arabic wording of the moon hadith and its literal translation. Notice the phrase 'as you see the moon' and the variant readings at the end.
Pay attention to the footnotes for linguistic details.
2
Identify the point of the simile
Sanusi explicitly states that the purpose of comparing the vision to the moon is 'the lack of jostling and harm.' Find this in the text and understand why he emphasizes this.
3
Understand what is negated
Sanusi denies that direction, corporeality, or physical illumination are intended by the simile. Reflect on why these attributes are impossible for Allah.
This is the key to preserving tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى : ( تَعْرِفُ فِي وُجُوهِهِمْ نَضْرَةَ النَّعِيمِ)
وأما السنة : فأحاديث كثيرة ، مشهورة مستفيضة :
منها : حديثُ : سَتَرَوْنَ رَبَّكُمْ كَمَا تَرَوْنَ الْقَمَرَ لَيْلَةَ الْبَدْرِ « لا تَضَامُونَ - أو : لا تُضَارُّونَ - فِي رُؤْيَتِهِ (۱) ، ووجه التشبيه بالقمر : قد أشار إليهِ آخرُ الحديث ؛ وهو عدم ازدحامهم وتضار بعضهم ببعض وقت الرؤية (٢) ، أمَّا الجهة والجسمية ولوازمهما ؛ كالاستنارة الحسيَّةِ ونحوها . . فليسَتْ مقصودةً بالتشبيه ؛ إذ هي مستحيلة على المولى جلَّ وعزّ .
(۱) رواه البخاري ( ٥٥٤ ) ، ومسلم ( ٦٣٣ ) من حديث سيدنا جرير بن عبد الله رضي الله عنه ، والبخاري ( ٧٤٣٧ ) ، ومسلم (۱۸۲ ) من حديث سيدنا أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه ، وروي بتخفيف الراء في ( تضارون ) وتخفيف الميم في ( تضامون ) . (۲) التضارُّ : من الضرر ؛ أي: لا يضرُّ بعضهم بعضاً ، وهو لازم عن نفي التضام أيضاً .
The Exalted said: 'You will recognize in their faces the radiance of bliss.' As for the Sunnah: there are many hadiths, well-known and widespread. Among them is the hadith: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon; you will not crowd one another — or: you will not harm one another — in seeing Him (1). And the point of the comparison to the moon was indicated by the end of the hadith: it is the lack of jostling and some of them harming others at the time of vision (2). As for direction, corporeality, and their concomitants, such as physical illumination and the like, these are not intended by the simile, for they are impossible for the Lord, Glorified and Exalted.
(1) Narrated by al-Bukhari (554) and Muslim (633) from the hadith of our master Jarir ibn 'Abdullah, may Allah be pleased with him, and by al-Bukhari (7437) and Muslim (182) from the hadith of our master Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, and it has been narrated with lightening of the rā' in (tudārūn) and lightening of the mīm in (tadāmūn). (2) al-taḍārru: from al-ḍarar, meaning: one does not harm another, and it is a necessary consequence of the negation of crowding.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage presents the proof from the Sunnah for the vision of Allah (ru'yah) in the afterlife. The author first quotes a Quranic verse (Sūrat al-Insān 76:22) as a preface, then states that the Sunnah contains many widespread hadiths on this topic. He presents the famous hadith reported by Jarīr ibn 'Abdullāh and Abū Hurayra: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon.' The author immediately addresses a potential misinterpretation: the comparison to the moon is not about similarity in essence, location, or physical attributes. Rather, the point of the simile—as indicated by the end of the hadith ('you will not crowd one another')—is that the vision will be clear and unobstructed, with no jostling or harm. The author explicitly negates direction (jiha), corporeality (jismiyya), and their concomitants like physical illumination, affirming that these are impossible for Allah. He then provides a footnote with the chain of transmission and a linguistic note on the variant readings of 'tadāmūn' and 'tudārūn'.

Key Terms
رؤية
تشبيه
تضار
تضام
جهة
جسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith means Allah is similar to the moon in form, location, or having physical light.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the simile is only about the clarity of vision (no crowding), and that all physical attributes are impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
What is the intended purpose of the simile comparing the vision of Allah to seeing the moon?
A) To indicate that Allah has a physical form like the moon.
B) To emphasize that the vision is clear and without crowding.
C) To show that the vision occurs only during the full moon.
D) To prove that Allah is located in a specific direction.

Key Takeaway
The moon hadith proves ru'yah, but the likeness is only about the absence of crowding—never about essence or attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment, do you instinctively picture a physical form or direction? How does the end of this hadith correct that instinct, and what does that correction demand from your heart right now?
?
If you hold onto the belief that Allah resembles the moon in any way, what does that say about your understanding of tanzih and your worship? Will you surrender this imagination to the clear words of the scholars and the hadith itself?
?
The hadith promises a vision so clear that no one crowds or harms another. Does your longing for that vision shape how you treat others today, especially those who might 'crowd' your life? What is one concrete step you will take to remove jostling from your interactions?

The Hadith of Suhayb: Looking Upon the Face of Allah
Sanusi presents and interprets the hadith of Suhayb on the vision of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi interprets the hadith of Suhayb to affirm the vision of Allah while rejecting corporeality.

Core Idea
Seeing the 'face' of Allah means seeing His essence, not a physical face.

In this lesson, we examine one of the key prophetic hadiths cited by Sunnis for the beatific vision. Sanusi not only presents the hadith but also provides his own theological commentary to clarify that 'face' refers to the transcendent essence of Allah, free from any physical attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Note the footnote numbers; they are not part of the hadith text.
2
Examine Sanusi's commentary on key terms
Identify his explanations of 'ished', 'raising the veil', and 'looking upon the face'. Notice how each term is reinterpreted to avoid anthropomorphism.
He equates 'ished' with 'willed', not desire.
3
Reflect on divine transcendence
Consider why Sanusi insists that the vision is of the essence, free from body, direction, or place. This is a core principle of Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا هَذَا الْمَوْعِدُ ؟ أَلَمْ يُثَقِّلْ مَوَازِينَنَا ، وَيُبَيِّضُ وُجُوهَنَا ، وَيُدْخِلْنَا الْجَنَّةَ ، وَيُجِرْنَا مِنَ النَّارِ ؟! (۱) ، قال : « فَيُرْفَعُ الْحِجَابُ ، فَيَنْظُرُونَ إِلَى وَجْهِ اللهِ عَزَّ وَجَلَّ » ، قَالَ : ( فَمَا أُعْطُوا شَيْئاً أَحَبَّ إِلَيْهِمْ مِنَ «
«
النَّظَرِ (٢) .
قلت : ومعنى ( اشتهى ) في هذا الحديث : أَراد ، ومعنى رَفع الحجاب : إزالة المانع عن أعين الناظرينَ ، وخَلْقُ إدراكاتٍ لهم تتعلَّق بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ، ومعنى ( ينظرونَ إلى  َوجْهِ اللهِ ) : ينظرون إلى ذاتِهِ المنزَّهة عن الجسميَّةِ والأعضاء والجهة والمكان .
ومنها : قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : « إِنَّ أَدْنَى أَهْلِ الْجَنَّةِ مَنْزِلَةٌ لَمَنْ يَنْظُرُ إِلَى جِنَانِهِ وَأَزْوَاجِهِ وَنَعِيمِهِ وَخَدَمِهِ وَسُرُرِهِ مَسِيرَةَ أَلْفِ سَنَةٍ ، وَأَكْرَمَهُمْ عَلَى اللهِ مَنْ يَنْظُرُ إِلَى وَجْهِهِ عُدْوَةً وَعَشِيّاً (۳) .
قلتُ : يعني بالوجه : الذات ؛ لاستحالة الوجه ، بل مطلق العضو على الله تعالى .
(1) الاستفهام هنا للتقرير .
(۲) رواه مسلم ( ۱۸۱ ) ، والترمذي ( ۳۱۰۵ ) ، وابن ماجه ( ۱۸۷ ) ، وليس فيه لفظ ( اشتهى ) ، بل ( يريدُ ) وكذا سيفسره المصنف ، قال القاضي الباقلاني في » تمهيد الأوائل وتلخيص الدلائل » ( ص ٤٨ ) : ( فإن قال قائل : فهل تجوز عليه تعالى الشهوة ؟ قيل له : إن أراد السائل بوصفه بالشهوة الإرادة . . صحيح في المعنى ، غير أنه قد أخطأ وخالف الأمة في وصف القديم بالشهوة ؛ إذ لم يكن ذلك من أوصافه وأسمائه ) .
(۳) رواه الترمذي ( ٢٥٥٣ ) من حديث سيدنا ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما .
What is this appointment? Did He not make our scales heavy, make our faces white, admit us to Paradise, and grant us safety from the Fire?! (1). He said: 'So the veil will be lifted, and they will look upon the face of Allah, Mighty and Majestic.' He said: 'They were not given anything more beloved to them than the vision (2).'
I say: The meaning of 'ished' in this hadith is 'He willed'; the meaning of 'the raising of the veil' is the removal of the barrier from the eyes of the beholders, and the creation of perceptions for them that relate to His Essence, Glorified and Exalted; and the meaning of 'they look upon the face of Allah' is that they look upon His Essence, which is purified of corporeality, members, direction, and place.
And among them is his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Indeed the lowest of the people of Paradise in rank is one who will look at his gardens, spouses, bliss, servants, and thrones for the distance of a thousand years, and the most honored of them with Allah is one who looks at His face morning and evening (3).'
I say: By 'face' He means the Essence, because a face—nay, any member—is impossible for Allah, Exalted.
(1) The interrogative here is for affirmation. (2) Narrated by Muslim (181), al-Tirmidhi (3105), and Ibn Majah (187), and it does not contain the word 'ished' but rather 'yurīdu', and thus the author will interpret it. Al-Qadi al-Baqillani said in 'Tamhīd al-Awā'il wa Talkhīṣ al-Dalā'il' (p. 48): 'If someone says: Is desire (shahwa) permissible for the Exalted? It is said to him: If the questioner means by describing him with desire the will... that is correct in meaning, except that he has erred and contradicted the community in describing the Pre-Eternal with desire, for that was not among His attributes and names.' (3) Narrated by al-Tirmidhi (2553) from the hadith of our master Ibn 'Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage continues the sunnah evidence for the vision of Allah. The author presents the hadith of Suhayb: the people of Paradise ask about an appointment, and they are told that the veil will be lifted so they can look upon the face of Allah, and nothing is more beloved to them than that. The author then provides his own commentary (qultu): he clarifies that the word 'ished' (desired) in the hadith means 'willed' (arāda), and interprets 'raising the veil' as the removal of an impediment from their eyes and the creation of perceptions that relate to Allah's essence. Most importantly, he explains 'looking upon the face of Allah' as looking upon His essence (dhāt), which is transcendent beyond corporeality, members, direction, and place. Then he cites another hadith from Ibn 'Umar about the lowest and highest ranks in Paradise: the most honored look at Allah's face morning and evening. He again comments that 'face' here means essence, because a literal face or any member is impossible for Allah. The footnotes include a citation chain and a quotation from al-Baqillani affirming that 'desire' when attributed to Allah must be interpreted as will, not as a passion.

ذات
حجاب
رؤية
جسمية
عضو
جهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'face of Allah' refers to a literal physical face.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies it means the essence, which is devoid of organs and direction.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does 'looking upon the face of Allah' mean?
A) Seeing a literal face with eyes.
B) Looking at a direction in the sky.
C) Looking upon His essence, free from physical attributes.
D) Observing a reflection in Paradise.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi interprets the vision of Allah's face as the vision of His transcendent essence, not a physical sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly long to see Allah's essence, or are you more attached to the fleeting pleasures of this world? What would it take for that longing to become the greatest desire of your heart?
?
Sanusi teaches that the 'face' of Allah is His transcendent essence, not a physical form. Does your heart still imagine Allah in a limited way, with direction or place? How can you purify your conception of Him?
?
The removal of the veil requires the creation of perceptions in you. Are you actively purifying your inner self so that you may be capable of that vision, or are you filling your heart with distractions that thicken the veil?

The Hadith of Ibn 'Umar: The Highest Rank in Paradise
Sanusi cites and interprets the hadith of Ibn 'Umar on the highest vision in Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi applies the same interpretive principle to the hadith of Ibn 'Umar, equating 'face' with essence.

Core Idea
In Paradise, the highest honor is seeing Allah's face, which means His essence.

This lesson presents a second hadith that describes the ranks of Paradise and the special honor of looking at Allah's face twice daily. Sanusi again emphasizes that 'face' must be understood as the essence, since a literal face is impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the comparison between the lowest and highest ranks.
The hadith highlights the superiority of seeing Allah's face over all other delights.
2
Examine Sanusi's concise comment

He does not elaborate further; the principle is already clear from the previous lesson.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on the consistency of Sanusi's method: all anthropomorphic expressions about Allah are interpreted as referring to His essence.

The Veil and the Vision: Interpreting the First Hadith
Author cites hadith and interprets its key terms to affirm vision while negating corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author interprets the hadith of the veil being lifted to affirm the vision of Allah while maintaining His transcendence beyond body, limbs, direction, and place.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is real, but not with physical eyes—it is a direct perception of His essence.

In this passage, Imam Sanusi presents the first transmitted proof for the vision of Allah in the afterlife: a well-known hadith where the Prophet describes the lifting of the veil and the believers looking at the 'face' of Allah. This hadith could be misinterpreted as implying a physical face or direction. Sanusi immediately clarifies the meaning in a way that upholds divine transcendence (tanzih). Let's read his explanation carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its context
Begin by reading the hadith as quoted by Sanusi. Notice the initial question about the promise of Paradise and the Prophet's response about the veil being lifted.
The hadith emphasizes that the vision is the most beloved thing given to the believers.
2
Identify the three terms explained
Sanusi provides explanations for three key terms: 'desire' (ishtihā'), 'lifting the veil' (raf' al-hijāb), and 'looking at the face' (al-nazar ilā wajh Allāh). Find each one in the text.
3
Understand each interpretation
For each term, note how Sanusi removes any corporeal implication. 'Desire' is reinterpreted as will; 'lifting the veil' is creating perceptions; 'face' is the essence, free from body, limbs, direction, and place.
This is the core of the lesson—affirming the vision while negating anthropomorphism.
4
Reflect on the balance
Consider why Sanusi insists on these reinterpretations. How does this balance the literal meaning of the hadith with the principle of tanzih?
This reflects the Ash'ari method of interpreting ambiguous texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مَا هَذَا الْمَوْعِدُ ؟ أَلَمْ يُثَقِّلْ مَوَازِينَنَا ، وَيُبَيِّضُ وُجُوهَنَا ، وَيُدْخِلْنَا الْجَنَّةَ ، وَيُجِرْنَا مِنَ النَّارِ ؟ قال : « فَيُرْفَعُ الْحِجَابُ ، فَيَنْظُرُونَ إِلَى وَجْهِ اللهِ عَزَّ وَجَلَّ » ، قَالَ : « فَمَا أُعْطُوا شَيْئاً أَحَبَّ إِلَيْهِمْ مِنَ النَّظَرِ » . قلت : ومعنى اشتهى في هذا الحديث : أَراد ، ومعنى رَفع الحجاب : إزالة المانع عن أعين الناظرينَ ، وخَلْقُ إدراكاتٍ لهم تتعلَّق بذاتِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ، ومعنى ينظرونَ إلى وَجْهِ اللهِ : ينظرون إلى ذاتِهِ المنزَّهة عن الجسميَّةِ والأعضاء والجهة والمكان . ومنها : قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : « إِنَّ أَدْنَى أَهْلِ الْجَنَّةِ مَنْزِلَةٌ لَمَنْ يَنْظُرُ إِلَى جِنَانِهِ وَأَزْوَاجِهِ وَنَعِيمِهِ وَخَدَمِهِ وَسُرُرِهِ مَسِيرَةَ أَلْفِ سَنَةٍ ، وَأَكْرَمَهُمْ عَلَى اللهِ مَنْ يَنْظُرُ إِلَى وَجْهِهِ عُدْوَةً وَعَشِيّاً » . قلتُ : يعني بالوجه : الذات ؛ لاستحالة الوجه ، بل مطلق العضو على الله تعالى . وأما الإجماعُ : فلا خفاءَ أَنَّ السلف الصالح معلومٌ مِنْ حالِهمُ الرغبة إلى الله تعالى أن يُمتِّعَهُم بالنظر إليه جل وعزَّ . وبالجملة : فثبوتُ الرؤيةِ يكادُ أنْ يكونَ مِمَّا عُلِمَ مِنَ الدِّينِ ضرورةً . نسأله سبحانَهُ ألا يحرمنا مِنَ النظر إليه نظرَ أَهلِ الخصوصِ مِنْ أوليائه ، وأهل معرفتِهِ ، وأكرم أهلِ الجنَّةِ عليه ، بجاهِ سيدنا ومولانا محمد صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ وشرَّفَ وكرَّمَ ، ثم بجاه ملائكته وجميع رُسُلِهِ وأنبيائه .
What is this promise? Did it not weigh down our scales, whiten our faces, admit us to Paradise, and save us from the Fire? He said: 'The veil is lifted, and they look at the face of Allah, Mighty and Majestic.' He said: 'They were not given anything more beloved to them than the vision.' I said: The meaning of 'desired' in this hadith is 'willed'; the meaning of 'lifting the veil' is removing the barrier from the eyes of the beholders and creating perceptions for them that relate to His essence, Exalted and Majestic; and the meaning of 'they look at the face of Allah' is that they look at His Essence, which is transcended beyond corporeality, limbs, direction, and place. Among these (proofs) is his saying, peace and blessings be upon him: 'Indeed, the lowest of the people of Paradise in rank is one whose view of his gardens, spouses, bliss, servants, and couches extends for a thousand years, and the most honored of them to Allah is one who looks at His face morning and evening.' I said: He means by 'face' the Essence, because the (literal) face, indeed any limb, is impossible for Allah, Exalted. As for consensus: there is no hidden fact that the righteous predecessors, it is known from their state, desired Allah, Exalted, to grant them the vision of Him, Mighty and Majestic. In summary: the reality of the vision is almost something known from religion necessarily. We ask Him, Glorified, not to deprive us of the vision of Him, the vision of the people of special closeness among His saints, the people of His knowledge, and the most honored of the people of Paradise with Him, by the rank of our master and lord Muhammad, may Allah bless him, honor, and grant him peace, and by the rank of His angels and all His messengers and prophets.
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage presents the transmitted evidence for the vision of Allah (ru'yah) in the afterlife. The author begins by citing a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ asks about the promise of Paradise, then describes the lifting of the veil and the believers looking at the face of Allah. The author then provides his own commentary (qultu) to interpret key terms in a way consistent with divine transcendence (tanzih): 'desire' means will, 'lifting the veil' means removing the barrier from the eyes and creating perceptions related to His essence, and 'face' means His essence (dhāt) free from corporeality, limbs, direction, and place. He then cites another hadith distinguishing the lowest and highest ranks of Paradise, where the highest is looking at Allah's face morning and evening. He again explains that 'face' means essence, since a literal face or any limb is impossible for Allah. Next, he invokes consensus (ijmā') of the righteous predecessors (salaf), whose known desire for the vision confirms its reality. He concludes that the reality of the vision is almost known necessarily from religion. The passage ends with a supplication asking Allah for the vision of the elite among His saints.
